and third columns (A, C, E, G, I, K, M, O) are plots controlling for assessment center and genotype array
in the GWAS. The second and fourth columns (B, D, F, H, J, L, N, P) contain plots without controlling
for assessment center and genotype array in the GWAS, taken from the main text or supplementary
text for comparison. At the group level (left two columns), we measured prediction accuracy with the
squared partial correlation between the PGS and the trait value in 250 bins of 278 individuals each.
At the individual level (right two columns), we measured prediction accuracy as the squared difference
between the predicted phenotype and the true phenotype value. At both the group and individual levels,
y-axis values show relative prediction accuracy, i.e. prediction accuracy divided by a baseline value. The
baseline value is the mean prediction accuracy in 25 bins with median genetic distances that are closest
to the mean genetic distance for GWAS individuals. Supplementary Table 1 details these baseline values
for each trait. Curves show cubic spline fits, with 8 knots placed based on the density of data points.
For all panels, no repeated measurements from the same individual were taken.

. The second and

details these baseline values for each trait. Curves show cubic spline fits, with 8 knots placed based on
the density of data points. For all panels, no repeated measurements from the same individual were
taken.

This figure presents the same analysis as Fig. 2 and Supplementary Fig. 5, but the first and third columns
(A, C, E, G, I, K, M, O) are plots with GWAS run with regenie 2
. The second and fourth columns (B, D,
F, H, J, L, N, P) contain plots with GWAS run with PLINK11, taken from the main text or supplementary
text for comparison. At the group level (left two columns), we measured prediction accuracy with the
squared partial correlation between the PGS and the trait value in 250 bins of 278 individuals each.
At the individual level (right two columns), we measured prediction accuracy as the squared difference
between the predicted phenotype and the true phenotype value. At both the group and individual levels,
y-axis values show relative prediction accuracy, i.e. prediction accuracy divided by a baseline value. The
baseline value is the mean prediction accuracy in 25 bins with median genetic distances that are closest
to the mean genetic distance for GWAS individuals. Supplementary Table 1 details these baseline values
for each trait. Curves show cubic spline fits, with 8 knots placed based on the density of data points.
For all panels, no repeated measurements from the same individual were taken.

accuracy, i.e. prediction accuracy divided by a baseline value. The baseline value is the mean prediction
accuracy in 25 bins with median genetic distances that are closest to the mean genetic distance for GWAS
individuals. Supplementary Table 1 details these baseline values for each trait. Curves show cubic spline
fits, with 8 knots placed based on the density of data points. For all panels, no repeated measurements
from the same individual were taken.

Supplementary Figure 61: Trends of portability, with PGS constructed using PRS-CS, for other
biomarkers. This figure presents the same analysis as Fig. 2 and Supplementary Fig. 5, but the first and
third columns (A, C, E, G, I, K, M, O) are plots with PRS reconstructed with PRS-CS 3
. The second and
fourth columns (B, D, F, H, J, L, N, P) contain plots with PGS constructed with PLINK11, taken from
the main text or supplementary text for comparison. At the group level (left two columns), we measured
prediction accuracy with the squared partial correlation between the PGS and the trait value in 250 bins
of 278 individuals each. At the individual level (right two columns), we measured prediction accuracy as
the squared difference between the predicted phenotype and the true phenotype value. At both the group
and individual levels, y-axis values show relative prediction accuracy, i.e. prediction accuracy divided by
a baseline value. The baseline value is the mean prediction accuracy in 25 bins with median genetic
distances that are closest to the mean genetic distance for GWAS individuals. Supplementary Table 1
details these baseline values for each trait. Curves show cubic spline fits, with 8 knots placed based on
the density of data points. For all panels, no repeated measurements from the same individual were
taken.

Supplementary Figure 67: Portability, as a function of both genetic distance and Townsend Deprivation Index, for anthropometric measurements. We first stratified the prediction sample individuals
into equal-sized strata of Townsend Deprivation Index: 2 strata at the group level, shown on the left,
and 5 equal-sized strata at the individual level, shown on the right panels. Within each stratum, we
then performed the same analysis of prediction accuracy as a function of genetic distance as in Fig. 2
and Supplementary Fig. 2. At the group level, we measured prediction accuracy with the squared partial
correlation between the PGS and the trait value in 250 bins of 278 individuals each. At the individual

accuracy in 25 bins with median genetic distances that are closest to the mean genetic distance for GWAS
individuals. Curves show cubic spline fits, with 8 knots placed based on the density of data points. Top
represents those who are more deprived. See Fig. 3C for the individual level of BMI. For all panels, no
repeated measurements from the same individual were taken.

for GWAS individuals. Curves show cubic spline fits, with 8 knots placed based on the density of data
points. Top represents those who are more deprived. For all panels, no repeated measurements from the
same individual were taken.

Supplementary Figure 70: Portability, as a function of both genetic distance and Townsend Deprivation Index, for other biomarkers. We first stratified the prediction sample individuals into equal-sized
strata of Townsend Deprivation Index: 2 strata at the group level, shown on the left, and 5 equal-sized
strata at the individual level, shown on the right panels. Within each stratum, we then performed the
same analysis of prediction accuracy as a function of genetic distance as in Fig. ?? and Supplementary
Fig. 5. At the group level, we measured prediction accuracy with the squared partial correlation between
the PGS and the trait value in 250 bins of 278 individuals each. At the individual level, we measured
prediction accuracy as the squared difference between the predicted phenotype and the true phenotype
value. At both the group and individual levels, y-axis values show relative prediction accuracy, i.e. prediction accuracy divided by a baseline value. The baseline value is the mean prediction accuracy in 25
bins with median genetic distances that are closest to the mean genetic distance for GWAS individuals.
Curves show cubic spline fits, with 8 knots placed based on the density of data points. Top represents
those who are more deprived. For B, when plotting the spline fit on a logarithmic scale, we replaced the
non-positive values with 10−6
. For all panels, no repeated measurements from the same individual were
taken.

Supplementary Figure 71: Measuring genetic distance from the GWAS sample, with White British
individuals included in the prediction set. This figure mirrors main text Fig. 1C, but reflects different
compositions of the GWAS sample and the prediction sample than the ones used for the main text
analysis. Here, after filtering, from a total of 336,923 WB individuals, we randomly selected 300,000 as
the GWAS set, and used the remaining 106,423 individuals as the prediction set (36,923 WB and 69,500
NWB). We followed the same procedures as described in Methods, and identified that across 10,000
individuals from the prediction set, genetic distance to the GWAS sample (calculated with 40 PCs) is
highly correlated with Fst between the GWAS sample and the individual. For reference, we show the
mean genetic distances for subsets of the 1000 Genomes dataset 12: CEU, Utah residents of primarily
Northern and Western European descent; CHB, Han Chinese in Beijing, China; YRI, Yoruba in Ibadan,
Nigeria. The dashed line represents the 97.5
th percentile of genetic distance from among GWAS sample
individuals. The inset is a zoomed-in view of genetic distances up to 3.0, on a log scale, to better visualize
the distribution of the new GWAS sample. For all panels, no repeated measurements from the same
individual were taken.

Supplementary Figure 72: A comparison of portability trends, with different choices of the GWAS
and prediction samples, for anthropometric measurements. This figure presents the same analysis as
Fig. 2 and Supplementary Fig. 2, but the first and third columns (A, C, E, G, I, K, M, O) show results
obtained with a smaller GWAS set of 300,000 individuals labeled by the UKB as White British (WB)
and a larger prediction set of 36,923 WB and 69,500 additional individuals not labeled as White British
(NWB). The second and fourth columns (B, D, F, H, J, L, N, P) show results obtained with a GWAS
set of 336,923 WB and a prediction set of 69,500 NWB only. At the group level (left two columns),
we measured prediction accuracy with the squared partial correlation between the PGS and the trait
value, in 382 bins of 279 individuals each in the first column and 250 bins of 278 individuals each in
the second column. At the individual level (right two columns), we measured prediction accuracy as the
squared difference between the predicted phenotype and the true phenotype value. At both the group
and individual levels, y-axis values show relative prediction accuracy, i.e. prediction accuracy divided
by a baseline value. The baseline value is the mean prediction accuracy in 38 bins with median genetic
distances that are closest to the mean genetic distance for GWAS individuals. Curves show cubic spline
fits, with 8 knots placed based on the density of data points. For all panels, no repeated measurements
from the same individual were taken.
